0439023521|3.0|Everyone around me has been talking about this book and I finally got a chance to read it.  I burned through it in less than a weekend, but I found myself wanting to like it more than I actually did.  The plot went extremely fast (and normally I read quite slow), but to me there was no depth to the story.  Each revelation the main character had seemed forced to me.  I felt like I was constantly being told this or that fact was important,  or the main character felt this way without the prose actually making me *feel* that way.  It was just too much telling and not enough showing.  Also, the dialogue throughout (epsecially between Peeta and Katniss) felt so stilted and rang very untrue to me.  Often sentences were confusing and sometimes scenes transitioned so quickly it was confusing I had to read over a passage a few times.  (It seemed on more than one occasion that Katniss had spent her last arrow, when in the next scene she was reloading her bow.)  There was even a typo in my copy.  ***SPOILERS***  I was expecting a more literary story (i.e. more thematic symbols--more could've been done with the mockingjay pin and the fire symbology, both missed opportunities on Collins's part.  The ending was a pure cop out: more could've been done to push the characters to their absolute limits and examine how far one really would go for survival.  It seems counterintuitive to say so, but there were so many cop outs in this plot, so many \\"deus ex machinas\\", that I grew frustrated with the believability of the plot.  Just when things seemed to get really juicy, the diffcult option was removed from Katniss's (and Peeta's) choice of options--every time she was in a position to have to kill a character she cared about, some outside force came in and killed them off.  (Rue, Thresh, Foxface.  Even Cato's death was more of a mercy killing.)  Imagine how profound the story would've been if she had to have allied with Rue and then have been forced to kill her in cold blood later on.  That's exactly what I was expecting when I started reading this book--I couldn't imagine it any other way than that she'd bond with the other children and then be forced to kill them, and then deal with those emotions.  Instead she got off the hook emotionally each time, and the story just felt flat to me because of it.  I really wanted the story to push her to her psychological limits in addition to physical limits.  She just made out too easily for a dystopian story centered around kids killing each other.  If she had been forced to kill Peeta, the story would've had some real weight to it.  (Though I suspect he's been kept alive mostly to set up a love triangle in the next book.)  The \\"stay tuned for Book Two!\\" ending only added to the frustration.  There is just no comparison to 1984 or The Giver, stories with real emotional weight, philosophical ideas, and more artfully-crafted prose.  I suppose the trend now is to have supposedly \\"dark\\" stories that are only dark in regards to action and plot, but lack any true psychological weight, and whose characters act coolly under pressure (except for a few obligatory tears) and emerge from the story unscathed in any profound sense.  (Who can forget Winston turning into coward at the end of 1984 and how his character is broken as a result?)  All in all, I feel this book had nothing to say about humanity.  There were glimmers of a theme, especially from the mouth of Peeta when he talks about dying his own terms, and when Katniss makes her first kill--but those themes are dealt with so briefly and then it's back to the action.  Peeta and Katniss feel more superficial towards the end of the story--I can't get any real sense of how they've changed after the Hunger Games, if at all.  We are only told in the exposition that Katniss feels different, but we are never quite shown how.  Same goes throughout the book--we are told some things are significant, but without any backstory, we can't feel how important they are.  Why is Katniss's decorating Rue's body with flowers such an act of defiance?  We are told after the fact that communication between Districts is forbidden.  Had we been told (or better yet, shown) that *beforehand*, the scene would've played differently.  There were just too many examples of events happening, then being told they were important after the fact.  Considering this is a best-seller, I was really surprised at the low quality of the writing style, even in regards to such basic things as scene transitions.  There were also a few elements that just seemed thrown in--like the muttations, that took more away from the story than added to it.  And must every YA book these days be written in first-person present tense?  A real disappointment and missed opportunity.  I really wanted it to be better after all the good things I heard about it.

1400052181|5.0|THE IMMORTAL LIFE OF HENRIETTA LACKS is an exceptional book on many levels. I agree with all who have stated what a remarkable job Rebecca Skloot did with what could have been dry material.  The most remarkable aspect of this book was that she successfully demonstrated that right down to the cellular level, EVERY PERSON is an individual. Every person has people they love, and those who love them. Everyone has feelings, and worth. She shows us it would be easy to sink into a debate about the monetary value of a human life, but there is no finite value to the infinite generations who will benefit from the tragic loss of Henrietta's family. They lost their mother, sister, cousin, wife. There is not a dollar amount that will ever give her back. It's a slippery slope to think that we can offer a person money for a life.  Henrietta died in a terrible amount of pain, and her suffering was horribly real. The cells she gave without consent or knowledge have since helped countless people to avoid that pain. A reader can speculate if the doctors approached her and told her, \\"We have no cure today, but the cells from your tumor may prevent other people from suffering this same fate, may we have them?\\" Henrietta might have agreed.  Skloot shines a light on a terrible era in our country, and yet how brightly the human spirit still shines in the face of such tragedy.  Her decade long passionate work has really payed off here.  She helps the reader to understand they didn't experiment on her. The medical staff used the most state of the art treatment they had available to try to help her. This is evidenced by the fact that the people who administered the treatment believed in it so much they suffered the consequences of handling the radium themselves.  Skloot's research revealed that people don't engage in the scientific pursuit of \\"finding a cure\\" for the sake of making mountains of money. They want the cure because they have an understanding at some level how painful loss reallyis. Individuals come up with the desire to stop some horrific disease and all the pain that disease brings, and they approach large companies with the hope of gaining funding for their research.  The money for funding comes from successes these companies have had in the past. If the treatment turns out to be detrimental, these companies suffer the tremendous loss (including the loss of jobs of all who worked for them: families....individuals.....relatives and friends suffer alike).  Rebecca Skloot is an example for us all. She looked beyond the wonder of the sheer numbers of cells, and looked for the person behind the cells. She gave Henrietta back her name, and has helped countless people who handle human cells to know that each culture had a person behind it. A real person with a real family who suffered real loss. Henrietta has moved from being a clinical observation back into being the person she will always be.  Deborah Lacks is said to believe her mother lives on in every person her mom touches. Henrietta Lacks touches countless people and still speaks to us today. Henrietta Lacks puts a face on every cure she's had her cells in, and now will touch the millions who read her story.

The script is by Christopher Hampton, and I wish more context wouul've been provided as to the moment in history that these events are taking place. But in the end, you will marvel at the acting performances in this movie. Here he goes completely the opposite direction and brings a restrained, if not understated, but beautiful performance as Jung. How is it possible that Fassbender did not get a single Oscar nomination for either performance? It blows the mind. All that aside, \\"A Dangerous Method\\" is a nice little movie, but beware, not for anyone in a hurry or wanting to see an \\"action\\" movie.
B0064NTZJO|5.0|We know him as Driver.  He doesn't carry a gun; he drives.  That's his job, and he's good at it.  Why?  Because it's a job, plain and simple.  But then he makes the ultimate mistake: he makes it personal.  And now people have to pay.<br /><br />There's a beautifully misleading simplicity about DRIVE.  It isn't what you think.  Behind Ryan Gosling's emotionless face is a deep well of emotions: anger, disgust, amusement, empathy.  Gosling is perfect in the part, conveying equal parts machismo, rage, and compassion.  He doesn't say much, but he doesn't need to: Driver is a man of action, not words.  As his quasi-romantic interest, Carey Mulligan again dazzles.  With a lesser cast and crew, her role would be shallow, merely there to serve the plot; she makes it three-dimensional, her own.  The same goes for the various supporting players: Bryan Cranston, Oscar Isaac, Christina Hendricks, James Biberi, and Ron Perlman.  Kudos especially to Albert Brooks, who makes arguably the most plausible charming-to-sadistic transition in recent cinema.  Props must also be given to the ever-steady direction of Nicolas Winding Refn, and the above-par script by Hossein Amini (who penned the unfairly overlooked KILLSHOT).<br /><br />DRIVE isn't a film for everyone.  It's a slow-burning noir in the old fashioned style: an anti-hero who's as much villain as anything; a dame in distress who's really tough-as-nails (but still beautiful and vulnerable); a cast of characters who are mostly unlikable.  It'll certainly be a disappointment to anyone whose short attention span has been captivated by the various FAST AND FURIOUS films.  (You hear about the person who sued because they thought it was an action film?  Wonder if it's the same person who was surprised at how hot their McDonald's coffee was.)  But for those with patience, and appreciation for subtlety and nostalgia, DRIVE is the film you've been waiting for.
B000FCKC4C|4.0|There are some works of fiction that are easy to pick apart and chew up because, being mostly culled from imagination, bits and pieces may not add up. This isn't one of those books. Like a piece of art that has endeared itself to one's heart such that its subsequent incarnations need not hide their flaws anymore, one does not come into this book with the intellect of a recent literature graduate to proselytize plots, settings, characterizations, misplaced commas and typeface (or whatever literature graduates quibble about).  The book is peppered with references to omens and Personal Legends. It makes you wonder whether life really wants you to achieve your Personal Legend or if there's a way to make sense of all the chaos. Whether haphazardly scribbled notes would someday turn into a novel. It gives you hope.  At some point, it stops being the kind of literature that has to conform to any sort of formality. It just is and what a wonderful feeling it is not to have to nitpick on something just to show that you analyze better than everyone else. Somewhere along the way, it became so that I wasn't just reading; I was also going over my own life, wondering where I'm headed, whether I'm also following my own Personal Legend.  The whole book speaks so voluminously that if I were looking for just one particular thing that calls out to me, I'd be in for a surprise deluge. At every turn, there is a passage that strikes a blow. From there, I started to doubt whether I was merely letting myself get carried away. It is a wordless form of doubt that later formed itself into a question: \\"Are you so jaded and old that you've forgotten what it's like to dream?\\" And so, even in my attempt to get away from my own emotions, I am pulled back to them.  If I were to begrudgingly assign errors (a human frailty I've perfected over the years), I would say that all characters speak like each other. Even the non-human characters that are allocated their own speaking lines speak the same way.Again, that Personal-Legend-hunter in me would ascribe that to a reason: Perhaps, we are all a part of each other. And why does everyone seem to believe in omens and Personal Legends? There is an answer to that as well: We meet people whom we need to meet, those who will fire us up to pursue our dreams.  I have a grudge with this book, too, though. It tends to be religious and biblical and I'm not a fan of anything that tends to convert. Still, I'm forced to ask myself, \\"Why is that so bad?\\" If I'm not going to be converted anyway, what is wrong with any piece of work attempting to do so?  In the end, that literary critic in me is curbed and I'm just so happy to let it stay silent.

0393064476|3.0|The book's ambitious subtitle is \\"How the World Became Modern,\\" yet the first 220 pages or so would better be characterized as a historically situated biography of Poggio Bracciolini, a bookhunter and Papal scribe whose main outstanding trait, as Greenblatt reminds us enthusiastically and repeatedly, is \\"beautiful handwriting.\\" The rest of the book is a catalogue of all the intellectual celebrities that possessed copies of De Rerum Natura, although the connection between the poem and their lives is tenuous for all but Montaigne. Not that I mind this: I'm fascinated by the Renaissance and the tale of book hunting and Papal scribes is an interesting vignette of the times.  Scientifically, there is no basis for believing that Lucretius' characterization of atoms was revolutionary or insightful, or that as Greenblatt congratulates his readers, \\"By now much of what On The Nature of Things claims about the universe seems deeply familiar, at least among the circle of people who are likely to be reading these words. After all, many of the work's core arguments are among the foundations on which modern life has been constructed.\\" While it is true that Lucretius, like many Greeks, entertained the idea of an 'atom,' it's clear that he had no scientific or empirical evidence for believing so, and that an educated person of the time would have no more reason to believe that matter consists of atoms than for believing it consisted of air, fire, water and earth, the other Greek theory of physics. It wasn't until 1805 that John Dalton, the English scientist, put forth a compelling theory for why atoms needed to exist. And even that perception of atom, which in Greek literally means \\"indivisible,\\" would be rendered literally false after the later scientific discoveries that atoms could be split, and furthermore that matter could be converted into energy.  Greenblatt seems to suggest that Lucretius's mention of atoms makes valid his other speculations on Epicureanism. He could have put more effort into following this path, to flesh out the impact of Epicurean thought on the modern world. As is, this thought is thinly sketched.  Stylistically, Greenblatt has the habit of trying to think out loud in other people's heads, as though they are characters in a novel: \\"Poggio could not possibly hope\\" (p. 129), \\"What he could not have grasped (p. 182). He engages in self-philosophizing, e.g. turn to p. 151 to find his definition of \\"the epitome of a failed life...\\" and he makes analogies that simply don't work all that well (\\"These relics, like the remains of long-dead insects, tell us what once buzzed about in the air of the Vatican\\", p. 142). His actual discussion of De Rerum Natura (starting in Chapter 8) is surprisingly light in textual quotation, and seems like a Cliffs Notes edition with heavy editorial redaction. It could have benefited from more directly engaging with the text. He frequently engages in the scholar's version of the tar and feather, tempering his excesses by appending something like \\"But of course there were exceptions\\" after making a sweeping generalization, usually negative. I found the discussion of his mother in the preface somewhat disturbing.  This book sheds light on an interesting era, and I especially enjoyed the connections made to modern textual archaeology, e.g. the recovered fragments from Herculaneum. However the writing is uneven and there are probably better places to read about how the world became modern.

B006PTL1QM|3.0|A DANGEROUS METHOD is a heavily intellectual work, with a heavy emphasis on sex...much like the work of Sigmund Freud, one of the main characters in t his fascinating yet aloof film.  The movie starts with a bang, as we see the clearly disturbed Sabina Spielrein (Keira Knightley) being forcibly admitted to a hospital.  She is thrashing and contorting her face and body in remarkable ways.  Surely this woman is in for a lengthy asylum visit.  But she is in fact going to be the first patient on which young Dr. Carl Jung (Michael Fassbender) will be trying his new method of Psychanalysis (yes, t that's what he calls it at first).  He places Spielrein in a chair and sits behind her and encourages her to talk.  Soon, she is confronting her demons and confessing some pretty lurid sexual feelings, including the pleasure she receives from humiliation and physical punishment.  From here, A DANGEROUS METHOD swings back and forth between being an interesting case study of the relationship between Jung and Spielrein and an intellectual tug of war between Jung, and his older mentor Sigmund Freud (Viggo Mortensen).  In the first instance, we see that the rigid divide between patient and doctor is not quite so settled in 1904.  Jung continues to treat Spielrein, but because she has a keen intellect and interest in her own treatment, he puts her to work as a research assistant.  And we also see their relationship teetering towards something even more than professor & student.  In the second instance, there are dinner table conversations and exchanged letters between Jung & Freud, wherein Jung challenges Freud to think beyond the confines of everything in psychoanalysis (as Freud calls it) relating to sex.  These Jung vs. Freud exchanges are INTERESTING.  They don't make for very exciting ENTERTAINMENT, however.  They feel a bit turgid.  Jung is, in many ways, such a fussbudget that he is incapable of small talk and only wants to challenge his mentor.  Freud, the elder statesman, isn't particularly inspired to fight back vigorously.  He is looking for a successor to himself and fancies that Jung will be such...but he also doesn't seem to embrace his role as inspiration and mentor.  He mostly seems vaguely bemused...at least until the relationship between Jung & Spielrein has a direct impact on the relationship between Jung & Freud.  The movie is certainly interesting in that much of it is true, including word for word readings of the correspondence between these two influential men.  But the movie fails to be emotionally gripping on almost any level.  The relationship between Jung & Spielrein is never gripping, as both of these people are driven by passions out of their control, and frequently come across as being acted UPON rather than making choices and deciding on actions.  And the relationship between Jung & Freud comes across like a well-acted reenactment in a TV documentary about the two men.  This is all the more disappointing, because with a director like David Cronenberg at the helm, I had hoped for more twisted sparks.  I think he was constrained by the stage-bound screenplay, and perhaps felt the emotional turmoil surely roiling in these characters would play more than it does.  It is simply too far below the surface most of the time.  Mortensen's role is fairly small, and we really only have the tiniest glimpse into Freud's life.  Fassbender is clearly a smart actor who understands the man he is playing on a very intellectual level, but what's missing is the sense that he is truly INHABITING his character...which is a shame, because Fassbender is such a terrifically physical actor.  Knightley has by far the flashiest role, and she is generally quite good.  Her character is the most interesting...and when reading the closing comments about what happens to each character...it's almost enough to make you want to read much more about her.  In many ways, this is the most \\"grown up\\" character Knightly has played.  She truly has a chance to mature into a woman.  A DANGEROUS METHOD is gorgeously filmed, and many of the locations are the actual locations these events took place.  The pace is fairly brisk.  It is never uninteresting...but I really wanted to care more about these people.  It's tough to actually recommend this film to a general audience...if the subject matter is of particular interest to you, then you should probably see it.

(2) The title.  \\"Morning Glory\\" seems like a Sunday or Christian or at least an innocent kind of phrase.  In reality there's a lot of worldly vulgarity and jerkiness in this movie that's not at all what the title might suggest(see #1). (3) Annoyance factor: some people might get annoyed by McAdams' frenetic energy throughout.  Though it's a personal preference, many might think she's a little too hyped up in this movie. (4) Her \\"romantic\\" relationship with Patrick Wilson is not developed at all, to the point that one thinks, why is it even in there?  They just have dinner (they say, but you have to take their word for it because it's not even shown) and jump into the sack.  Is that par for the course these days for twenty-somethings? Her friendship/work relationship with Harrison's character is really the focus of the story, but that's not romantic, and so this couldn't really be called a romantic comedy!  The pretty ...... Rachel McAdams of course.  Radiant actually, ifyou like to stare at pretty, high-energy women in their prime beauty who smile a lot.  (Great dimples!) Actually, Dianne Keaton also has a great, radiant smile for someone twice McAdam's age.  But McAdams really shines, and you can see that the director goes out of his way to film her beauty often, whether walking fast in stylish slit skirts or running in high heels.  So once again, some might see this movie as a fluffy plot vehicle to display Ms. McAdams personal charisma, beauty and charm.  And certainly compared to Harrison Ford's crotchety, vulgar and annoying sourness as a foil.

B005LAIIS0|3.0|THE GREY is an okay movie.  If I tell you the basic story, you'll think it's just a typical pulp action film...but the quality of cinematography, sound, script and acting elevates it to something a bit loftier.  As I watched it, I appreciated the effort the filmmakers were putting into this.  An element of existentialism that you can actually feel on an emotional level.  Yet at the same time, the film has people doing some incredibly stupid things, akin to what teenagers in slasher flicks might do.  Couching unexplainable behavior within an \\"artsy\\" framework doesn't make it any easier to swallow.  Liam Neeson is introduced working at a far northern Alaska installation.  His job is as a sniper of wolves, keeping the compound safe from these dangerous predators.  He's also contemplating suicide, but holds off at the last moment.  Thus, he ends up on an ill-fated flight from the installation.  The plane goes down in the middle of nowhere, leaving about seven survivors amidst the wreckage.  It is during these post-crash scenes that we truly get to see what a natural, physical leader Neeson's character is.  He barks orders that quickly organize the survivors and he sternly but gently attends the dying moments of a passenger as he bleeds out.  The death is looked upon in a series of powerful close-ups...and the stage is set that this isn't merely a movie in which a death is simple and unemotional.  The scene has power, and it also serves as the moment when the other survivors (for the most part) embrace the notion that Neeson is going to be their leader.  Soon, they discover that their wrecked fuselage is being stalked by man-eating wolves.  So Neeson decides that instead of waiting for possible but unlikely rescue in the plane and facing the wolves there, he suggests they grab what supplies they can and head for a tree line off in the distance, because he thinks the wolves MIGHT leave them alone if they think the men are leaving their territory.  This is a central decision, because most of the film follows thereafter...and yet it is not for ONE SECOND convincing.  It is stupid on the face of it.  Yes, wolves are stalking them...but they are in a location which provides some shelter from the HARSH wintry conditions, they have food and luggage to rummage through (which they never thoroughly do...who's to say someone wasn't carrying a satellite phone in their bag?) and it seems unlikely the wolves would swarm en masse into a giant steel structure.  And wouldn't large, ragged pieces of steel make excellent weapons against the wolves?  Instead, the men set out on foot in the hopes the wolves will leave them alone.  To say they made the wrong choice is understating matters (this is hardly a spoiler...it's what the film is ABOUT).  In my mind, Neeson is instantly established as a charismatic, tough-talking, tough-minded, physically adept FOOL.  Once they have made this choice, what follows are a series of man vs. man & man vs. nature encounters.  The characters are all nicely sketched, and some, such as the ex-con presented by Frank Grillo, are actually very well-rounded.  The men bond.  They fight.  They endure.  They make ever more desperate choices.  And the movie actually asks some big questions about death and facing death.  The manner in which we chose to go out...when given a choice.  As I said, it's more existential than you might expect.  There is less action and more contemplation that some viewers probably expect.  It's refreshing that the film would take the time to explore some of the moral quandaries it does.  And it ends more logically than I would have expected, given some of the dumb choices from earlier in the film.  I got past the terrible decision making early in the film, because I appreciated the excellent technical filmmaking throughout.  Director Joe Carnahan has given us a mean, unforgiving film...not a soft adventure where you can predict 10 minutes into it who will live and who will die.  But even with all the good things I just said, THE GREY is lacking.  Just little things.  The weather is incredibly cold, yet frostbite and exposed faces is a non-issue.  A wolf viciously attacks one man, yet when the wolf is fought off, the man has virtually no injuries...yet in an earlier, similar scene, the victim is torn apart.  There are just enough annoying inconsistencies, coupled with the one really bad initial decision to have pulled me out of really committing to this film.  It's ultimately a three-star, just barely recommend for me.

B000002L9M|3.0|I am puzzled as to why this is so consistently considered to be 'the' watershed Depeche Mode album, the pivotal moment in their career, the reference for everything before (\\"not bad for Pre-Black Celebration DM\\") to everything after it (\\"this is their best since Black Celebration\\"). To be sure, it's a good album, and some of the songs here are absolute classics in the DM canon. But for me, it's more of a slight variation in sound than the grand transformation it's touted to be.<br /><br />I've noticed that many people who worship Black Celebration think of DM's older work as dated. So, even if the preceding album, Some Great Reward, had other great qualities, the songs couldn't help sounding gimmicky, \\"tinny,\\" cheesy, etc. This, I think, is the main barrier that prevents people from realizing just how much of BC owes to Some Great Reward.<br /><br />You see, to me, Some Great Reward is that pivotal moment. The birth of most aspects of Depeche Mode's style that we have come to know and love. The early work is great too, but Some Great Reward saw the debut of a Depeche Mode that was dark, mysterious, theatrical, and sensual. Here, Dave Gahan became a much more expressive singer, ranging from a predatory croon to histrionic bellows, and Martin Gore provided the band's first tender ballad, not to mention his first stab at lead vocals. It's true that they were still enamored with synthesizers and samples, but the arrangements were much fuller and more atmospheric than in previous albums. There was sex, violence, cynicism, and dark drama. For all intents and purposes, Some Great Reward set the blueprint for almost every Depeche Mode album to come.<br /><br />Black Celebration's title track is a terrific opener, and has an effectively dour atmosphere to it, but its arrangement, especially that Philip Glass-like organ arpeggio, is clearly a variation of the one used for \\"Something to Do,\\" the opener for Some Great Reward. \\"Sometimes\\" is, to me, a lesser version of the original Gore ballad \\"Somebody,\\" with similar name and all. The closest song to a synth dance track on BC (New Dress) is like a moodier remix of \\"Master & Servant.\\" Even BC's best moment, the slow building dramatic heights of centerpiece \\"Stripped,\\" could have fit quite comfortably on Some Great Reward, alongside \\"If You Want\\" and \\"Blasphemous Rumors.\\"<br /><br />Still, Black Celebration does progress from the innovations of its predecessor. The main difference is the relative lack of samples and synth arrangements that formerly served as DM's stock in trade, in favor of a more expansive--albeit conventional--sound. A consequence of this is that it's rather light on the stomping anthems, and heavy on the ballads. I can appreciate that some people just like the constant bleak atmosphere of BC, but I can't understand how an album that is supposed to be Depeche Mode's defining moment has so few songs that actually make you want to move your body! No track to rival their earlier anthems, or later ones like \\"Strangelove\\" and \\"Personal Jesus.\\" It's true that the album feels cohesive, with a consistent moodiness running throughout. But it also lacks contrast, and its less distinctive moments (Dressed in Black, World Full of Nothing) make the album drag.<br /><br />Also, the claim that this is the first DM album that doesn't sound dated confuses me. Sure, the synth stabs of their first few albums scream 1980's, but so does the cavernous reverb that drenches ballads like \\"A Question of Lust.\\" That was a tried and true sound for ballads in the 80's. Perhaps this cavernous quality was intended as a reference to the early 60's sound of records produced by Phil Spector (the rhythm and castanets of The Ronettes'\\"Be My Baby\\" come to mind), but if that's the case, the accusation of being dated vs classic just seems to be an arbitrary distinction to me.<br /><br />To those of you who think Black Celebration's more conventional arrangements keep the songs from from being \\"cheesy,\\" lyricslike \\"death is everywhere, there are flies on the windscreen, for a start, reminding us we could be torn apart...tonight\\" should remind you that Depeche Mode have never escaped some level of cheesiness, even in Violator and beyond. Depeche Mode is known for their high drama, and to pull that off so consistently means that sometimes standards of good taste are violated. So if you're willing to put up with some silly lyrics once in a while, why can't you handle the synth stabs of Some Great Reward?<br /><br />I hate to rag on what is a decent album with some great songs, but the fact that this is so unquestionably regarded as their first classic album bothers me. The largest innovations that it brought relative to its predecessor (a move away from synth pop or industrial dance) are only important if you have hang-ups about early 80's synth sounds. I for one, relish the rhythms and textures of \\"classic\\" synth pop. Because of this, I am more open to the important innovations of Some Great Reward, and think of BC's own innovations as being a bit of a let-down, especially the lack of danceable anthems.<br /><br />A minority opinion, apparently, but one that I feel deserves consideration., for a start, reminding us we could be torn apart...tonight\\" should remind you that Depeche Mode have never escaped some level of cheesiness, even in Violator and beyond. Depeche Mode is known for their high drama, and to pull that off so consistently means that sometimes standards of good taste are violated. So if you're willing to put up with some silly lyrics once in a while, why can't you handle the synth stabs of Some Great Reward?  I hate to rag on what is a decent album with some great songs, but the fact that this is so unquestionably regarded as their first classic album bothers me. The largest innovations that it brought relative to its predecessor (a move away from synth pop or industrial dance) are only important if you have hang-ups about early 80's synth sounds. I for one, relish the rhythms and textures of \\"classic\\" synth pop. Because of this, I am more open to the important innovations of Some Great Reward, and think of BC's own innovations as being a bit of a let-down, especially the lack of danceable anthems.  A minority opinion, apparently, but one that I feel deserves consideration.

B00005Q3TZ|4.0|I saw this film when it first came to theaters a decade ago. From the trailers, it sold you as a modern retelling of the surprise attack on Pearl Harbor. What the film gives you however is a Hollywood production with Michael Bay helming it at the seat of his pants.  -- Film --  Most people here who have reviewed this film have already seen it. And you who probably seeks to understand why you want to buy this version of the film should consider a few things.  This film is a very fictional retelling of Pearl Harbor. When you watch this film, you need to remember that most (if not all) of what you see is either slightly inaccurate to greatly inaccurate. Does it mean that it makes it a bad film? From most people who are historical buffs or in general would like to see Hollywood pay respects to those service men who died during the attack - then yes.  But if you can take this film with a grain of salt and simply view it as is: Entertainment with the back drop being set in a historical setting. Then this film can be quite entertaining.  For me, I've studied this attack extensively as well as World War II itself and I know for a fact that this film is truly far from what had actually happened. Michael Bay - the film's director - it seemed was trying to convey a sense of realism through the telling of our two protoganists. By realism, I mean that the first act of the film takes place in a pre-war America.  Love is on everyone's minds as every service men and women are quite young. Michael Bay wanted to catch that moment in time where America was innocent and were not at all trying to jump on the board with the war that was going on around the world. In a sense, it worked.  The only problem is, Mr. Bay extends this sense of innocence between our two main protagonists who are both equally in love with the love interest. This love triangle is truly where it makes the film hit a few road blocks. Had he kept it between two people, then it would have been a minor rash on a film that was trying to keep with the time of that era.  Historical accuracy for the rest of the film it seems isn't the key in this film. Yes, Michael Bay keeps most things intact but are greatly twisted in the name of artistic license. During the attack sequence, in the Director's Cut, Michael Bay films it in a gritty fashion with emphasis on violence.  The violence in the attack sequence of Pearl Harbor is graphic in a very real sense and gives you a glimpse into what had happened during the attack. The Director's Cut is different from the theatrical cut that was seen in theaters. Graphic violence in which bodies and limbs are flying, bullets puncturing bodies with blood being something of a center-piece. From this, we get the prespective that Michael Bay wanted to show Pearl Harbor as it happened, however he misses the mark with some iffy writing from the first act and pure American justice (flaunted by some quick quips on friendship and American will power) in the final act before wrapping a dirty bow between the love triangle.  Bottom line is. While the film misses many marks, it still makes up for being a prime piece of entertainment that only Michael Bay could have delivered. The mere problem is, he chose the film to be set in a real historical event in which real people died.  -- Bonus Features --  I've seen most of the bonus features that are listed in the Director's Cut. Most of which are cutted into segments in which we get a sense of how the film was made. From boot camp to filming, Pearl Harbor it appeared was a titanic undertaking that Michael Bay took. There isn't just one documentary that takes you from the inception of the film to the end of production as I've enjoyed with many of Ridley Scott's works on his films. But it's enough to show you how this film was made.  -- In the End --  If you're seeking a film to quench your thirst on a historical epic with authentic precision. You're looking at the wrong film. If you're seeking a film that entertains with such high caliber entertainment - then pick this up. However, please remember that this film and others who are historically based should do their homework prior to watching films such as Pearl Harbor.

The issue of uncontrolled consumerism has driven the people to the earth to depleat all of its natural resources and look for raw materials elsewhere, in this case finding them in a near-by star system; a galactic take on the often repeated terrestrial story of exploitation.  Encountering an indiginous people on the planet Pandora, the human characters in the film begin to exploit them and their native land to their own, industrial ends.  Not unlike the history of genocidal conquest of the New World by western powers in search of gold, precious stones and land; the film demonstrates a disregard for the spiritual connection the natives have to their homeland in the minds of the foreigners.  The Na'vi live in a spritual place where they are connected to nature, which helps win the day in the final battle, through a neural connection between minds and souls of living things; this can be thought of as an alagory to several ancient or indiginous religious practices (though with a more scientific explanation in the modern era), with harmony and balance between people and nature being a central aspect to the belief system.<br /><br />A well-made and thought out movie, Avatar is both the chance to see some stunning special effects produced by one of the true masters of that field and the oportunity to stimulate a little thought on the historical perspective of industrialism's expansionistic past and the effects that it has had on local populations and their belief structures.

0812992792|5.0|This is an important, brilliantly conceived, though not always pleasant to read novel. At its center is Pak Jun Do, who withstands all sorts of brutality in the absurdly surreal world of North Korea. He goes from being an orphan, to a kidnapper, to a radio man on a fishing ship to a prisoner, and then finally assumes the identity of a national hero/tae kwondo champion who belongs to Kim Jung Il's inner circle and is the husband of the country's greatest national actress. The tales of how human life is so grotesquely devalued - from kidnapping innocent victims off Japanese soil to performing lobotomies on prisoners with nails over their eyeballs - make for difficult reading. But it remains a triumphant story about how this one character attempts to persevere, while everything he could hold precious, including his own identity, is stolen from him. The descriptions of the places he brings his mind to escape the physical torture or the things he must do to avoid starvation in prison camps, like eating moths, are delivered with brilliant prose. I read an interview with Adam Johnson in which he mentioned an upcoming memoir from someone who lived in a Korean gulag that details brutalities far in excess of any of those here. He also said he had to hold back and not match some of the more outlandish things Kim Jung Il actually did because it would have strained readers' credulity. It is hard to believe that a far more sadistic world than the one Orwell describes in 1984 actually exists somewhere, but this novel, in a magnificently literary way, offers a glimpse into just how cruelly distorted the world of North Korea is.

0345404475|3.0|Every so often I come across a book that is well known, well respected, so much so that when I read it I can't help but ask \\"What's all the fuss?\\" Sadly this is one of those books. I started reading it not caring too much that it was the source material for Blade Runner, I haven't seen that movie in over 25 years, but because it is often referred to as a Sci-Fi classic. Based on what I read, I don't know why, or how Philip K Dick came to be such a respected Sci-Fi writer. I didn't hate it, but I wasn't a classic either.  As many other reviews have noted, the author isn't much for heavy dialogue, descriptive action, or depth in his characters. I'm not familiar with any of the author's other works, so I don't know if this is his style, or if he was purposefully being vague in this book as sort of a tool to demonstrate the cold, impersonal nature of the world it is set in. Whatever his strategy, it didn't wow me. There are many moments that had the potential for frenzied action, tension building, or sense of satisfaction as you work through challenges with the protagonist Rick Deckard; but that potential was almost always wasted. Rick is as flat and nondescript as the androids he's hunting. You know he isn't in love with his job, but there isn't any back story to explain why he does it, or why his wife hates it (or much about his wife other than she's there and is a little weird about her moods), or even a general description of his appearance. None of the characters receive any real description accept for Rachael Rosen, who ironically sounds mannishly unattractive but is somehow an object of desire.  On the plus side something kept me reading all the way through. I've been known to drop a book I just hate (I'm looking at you Oscar Wao), but I at least wanted to know how the narrative resolved its self. Maybe I kept hoping I was missing something and the story would suddenly get deeper. The idea of semi-sentient androids trying to find freedom and meaning, even if it means murder is an interesting concept. I also thought the author's ideas about empathy being a distinguishing characteristic between humans and their creations was compelling, although I thought he could have gone to a deeper philosophical well with that. His androids are somewhat erratic, as well as demonstrating sociopathic behavior, but this is to be expected from something that thinks in purely logical modes. They are intelligent, but lack the experience, capacity and life span to put any of it to use beyond the desires of their creators. It at least makes the reader think a little. It isn't completely engrossing due to one blinding flaw: Humans would want machines that look and behave like them, but would want a way to ID them and shut them down as a safety precaution. If androids started murdering people and then escaping by blending into society, the consumers would demand \\"no-kill\\" programming, remote shut off, and easy means of identification.  Pros:  1.) The author's ideas about empathy are thought provoking. His uses of animals as something to give people something to feel a connection too after the Terminus War is also interesting; but I though he could have expanded on that.  2.) The androids are actually well thought out. They behave the way something would if they had little ability to emote beyond the simplest survival reflexes; they would kill or maim when necessary without remorse because it is merely a means to an end.  3.) The book is an easy read that won't consume too much of your time or brain power.  Cons:  1.) I read A LOT. Philip K Dick has some pretty tough competition in the form of the slew of fantasy, sci-fi and literary fiction authors I enjoy; but I find his powers of writing, at least in this case, somewhat wanting. If a book is going to be a \\"classic\\" I want depth, griping action and characters I want to know more about. There is little of that here.  2.) The whole Mercer thing went over my head. I understand the details of what theauthor described, but the details seemed too slapdash, so much so that the sort of big moments at the end didn't grip me at all. He should have stuck to the animals and expended on that concept.  3.) The very basic concept behind the plot of the book is unbelievably flawed. Humans may do some dumb things, but the first time an android killed someone or tried to pass themselves off as a human, that would be the end of that. As consumers, people flip out about silly little things, now imagine the backlash if something people paid over started killing people and ran off. Remember the firestorm over Toyotas that accelerated out of control? This would be that times a thousand. Sorry, not buying it.  Conclusion:  I can say I read this book and at least check that off my literary bucket list. It was OK, not great, and personally don't think a classic. It probably has an audience somewhere, but it isn't me.

B004LWZWC4|3.0|There has been a lot of buzz in the birding world when this movie was coming out. Almost every bird watcher that I know was anticipating its arrival. Our local Audubon chapter even brought out the author of the book to do a book signing and answer questions about this story. But was the hype really all it set out to be?  There are a few things this movie does a good job at - It entices people to go out and watch birds and shows just how challenging it can be and somewhat how fun. It does a good job at showing the different types of bird watchers (Laxed like Jack Black vs the very serious Owen Wilson), and it does an OK job at show casing the wide range of biodiversity (By geographic range only). But here's where it starts to get poor. The movie does not represent the book very well at all, it implicates a rather odd view of the pelagic trip leader, a person whom I've communicated with in real life Debrah Shearwater, it doesn't actually focus on the birds as much as the birding, it shows the extremes of bird watching and doesn't focus on the joys, and this movie can give birders a bad name (Now in state bird watching lists, we nickname certain birders Kamito based on their birding stratagies).  The book was very good and detailed, it covered the species being seen and chased ver well. The movie left a lot of the guts of this out. It added stuff that doesn't exist, and pretty much turned it into a hollywood movie, and not a true story like it truly is. While this is true for most books vs movies, I feel this hurt what could've been a better story in the long run, versus what it ended up being in reality. The casting was done very well, and Owen Wilson, Jack Black, and Steve Martin represent these three stereotypes based on their original characters, very well. So for that the movie is strong, but from the standpoint of a bird watcher with a dedicated life list for the US by ABA standards (Currently 445 species not a great accomplishment, but not bad after 3 years), and a world list by their own standards (687 currently), I was upset with the lack of details found by this movie.  If you go into it expecting to see a detailed report on Sandy Kamito's Big Year, you'll be slightly dissappointed. If you're doing this to see what kind of lives bird chasers live, you'll probably be more misled than educated. However, if you go into this with the mindset that I'm watching a movie that was written for  Hollywood and not for the story that sparked the book which led to the movie, you'll probably be happy with your purchase / rental and enjoy it. But please, don't take the movie to be a very serious representation for what really happened, and how birders really are. A Big Year is a very VERY tiny group of birders in the world. Not many people attempt it because it literally does take every ounce of your time and force you to spend a year traveling. This can be one of the most rewarding experiences in your life (Like for Jack Blacks character), or it can be the downfall of your existing life (Like Sandy Kamito - watch the movie to understand - no spoilers here).  I highly reccommend that if you read this, and what to know more about a real life Big Year experience, and the dedication, time, and money it takes to make this happen, hop over to google and search \\"John Vanderpoel Big Year 2011\\". John completed a big year this past December 31st missing the big year record by I believe 5 species (It's up in the air thanks to ambiguity in the ABA Guidelines for record acceptance - ask in the comments and I'll gladly explain). He of course won this last years record, and thanks to the remarkable migration flaws that occured, his number will more than likely be untouchable for the years to come, just as Kamitos still proves to be today.  In closure - this movie is just a glimpse at the 3 lives of 3 birders who have the same goal of copmeting in a big year. The movie will make you miss the days of traveling to Attu Alaska (no longer accessible to the public), provoke thoughts of traveling, but unfortunately it will do nothing to encourage the conservation of species, the protection of the birds habitats, or the pursuit for a species rights to continued existence. Maybe I expected too much, but as a birder, biologist, and wildlife conservationist, I was upset with the potential this movie had to make a difference.

145162445X|1.0|What a colossal bore this book would be if it didn't have a few interesting facts about the current state of quantum theory.  Unfortunately, the book's correct subtitle should have been not \\"Why There is Something Rather than Nothing\\" but only \\"How It Is That There Happens to Be This Something rather than Some Other Something.\\"  Admittedly that isn't quite the throat-grabber a publisher likes on a dust-jacket.  But--and why one has to point this out again and again and again to persons of a naturalist bent I cannot imagine--the transition from one physical state, describable by mathematical laws and comprising quantum functions, to another physical state is NOT the transition from nothing into a universe.  It leaves entirely untouched the venerable philosophical question of why there is something rather than nothing, and so the book is just one huge philosophical non sequitur.  I propose that henceforth that it should be legally required of any physicist who wants to write on ontological issues that he or she must first complete at least a Master's program in the history of philosophy and a Doctoral program in philosophical ontology.

At first, I didn't care too much about it, but the first time you have it drop out 3 times in a matter of a single minute, it's simply maddening. Imagine going randomly deaf for several seconds throughout every day of your life, and you'll get a sense of what it's like. Second problem is that the power button is a bit flaky. If the headset is plugged in via USB and powered off, sometimes it will not power on unless you unplug the headset and THEN power it on (and then you can plug it back in to continue charging). I thought it was a defect in my first headset until the same thing happened with the second one.  A minor third problem is that the boom mic is a little flimsy on the construction. I am careful with my toys, but it still cracked and finally snapped off one day when I was lowering the mic to use it, and that was only a few months into owning it.  OVERALL:  If they would fix the wireless, I would rate this 4 stars in a heartbeat. If they fixed the power button, it would go up to 5. That said, the G35 seems to be the G930 but wired, and cheaper. The number of times that I've wanted to really make good use of the wireless (go to another room and keep the audio going) is far less frequent than the dozens upon dozens of times that the audio cuts out, so it's just an unacceptable point for me, since that is what this headset is about.

0812415086|5.0|What strikes me most about the content of Anne Frank's diary is that in spite of her restricted living conditions, the thoughts and feelings she shares, those of a teenaged girl going through the mental and physical changes of puberty, are  a lot like those you'd expect of an ordinary girl. She writes about her somewhat strained relationship with her mother, Mrs. Van Daan (actually van Pels) and Mr. Dussel (who she gives a teasing sort of pseudonym, Fritz Pfeffer); her initial dislike for but eventual crush on annexmate, Peter Van Daan; and what she is reading and/or studying during the two years covered. Situation-specific topics include: the progress of the war, food, and events and interactions (with those on the outside who help them) related to their attempts to remain hidden.<br /><br />The first entry is dated June 12, 1942, her 13th birthday, on which she receives the diary that she calls  \\"Kitty.\\" She confides (p 2), \\"I want to write, but more than that, I want to bring out all kinds of things that lie buried deep within my heart,\\" and states that she starts it because she \\"has no such real friend.\\" Not yet in hiding, she lives with her parents and 16-year-old sister, Margot, and shares that they \\"emigrated to Holland in 1933.\\" She attends a Jewish school. Less than a month later, she writes (p 12), \\"Daddy began to talk to us of going into hiding,\\" and on July 5, 1942, wearing many layers of clothing, the family begins their life in the \\"Secret Annex,\\" part of the same building in which her father once worked. Eventually, eight persons inhabit the annex: the Franks, Mr. and Mrs. Van Daan, their 15-year-old son Peter, and the dentist, Mr. Dussel.<br /><br />What amazes me most about her writing is its eloquence and insightfulness. She reveals her feelings of inadequacy (p 46), \\"I frequently feel weak, and dissatisfied with myself; my shortcomings are too great. I know this, and every day I try to improve myself, again and again...in the end, I always come back to my diary. That is where I start and finish, because Kitty is always patient.\\" In spite of the circumstances, she keeps her chin up, writing (p 170), `And in the evening, when I lie in bed and end my prayers with the words, \\"I thank you, God, for all that is good and dear and beautiful,\\"' is hopeful (p 264), \\"I can feel the sufferings of millions and yet, if I look up into the heavens, I think that it will all come right that this cruelty too will end, and that peace and tranquility will return again,\\" yet realistic (p 64), \\"I could go on and on for hours about all the suffering the war has brought, but then I would only make myself more dejected. There is nothing we can do but wait as calmly as we can till the misery comes to an end. B004G5ZXVQ|4.0|Well it's only taken them two decades but REM have finally delivered what sounds like the logical follow up to 'Out Of Time' and 'Automatic For The People'. I always felt the lush textures of 'Out Of Time' and the personal introspection of 'Automatic...' were a perfect direction for the band to follow as they matured. In typical REM fashion they went their own way, but it's pleasing to see that after all the experimentation with noisy guitars, drum machines, keyboard loops and electronics, that they've finally returned to their signature sound.  Long-time REM fans, especially those who jumped on board with 'Green', will find plenty to love on 'Collapse Into Now'. The musical style is very much 'Out Of Time' crossed with 'Automatic...' with an added dash of 'Green' and 'New Adventures....', resulting in very much a \\"classic\\" sounding REM album. You know the deal: lots of jangling Rickenbackers, acoustic guitar, mandolin, and subtle keyboard and string touches, with a few rawer punchy rockers thrown in for good measure. As other reviewers have noted, this does mean that some songs here sound VERY similar to well-known tracks from earlier albums (e.g. 'Blue', 'Uberlin'), which may annoy some harsher judges but is not a big deal really. The key thing is that the album sounds really good, which more than makes up for a few familiar-sounding riffs.  What's most pleasing about 'Collapse Into Now' is its consistency. They don't have a standout hit single like 'Everybody Hurts' here, but they don't have a weak track either. You can pick any song: the statement-of-purpose opener 'Discoverer', the driving 'Mine Smell Like Honey', ballads like 'Uberlin', 'It Happenned Today' or 'Oh My Heart', or the eerie closer 'Blue'. Nothing sounds forced, out-of-place, or poorly executed, resulting in their most coherent musical and lyrical statement since 'New Adventures...'.  Above all, REM sound like they're relaxed and having fun again. The bands' post-'Automatic...' output often felt to me a bit forced and self-conscious, as if they were trying too hard to capture a specific \\"sound\\". 'Collapse Into Now' in contrast feels effortless; the band are back in their element and it shows.  So sure, you can criticise 'Uberlin' for sounding like 'Drive', or 'Blue' for sounding like 'E-Bow The Letter', or the one with the mandolin sounding the other ones with the mandolins. But you're kind of missing the point. REM finally want to sound like REM again, and on 'Collapse Into Now' they produce a classic album that serves as both a perfect end to their career, as well as a strong album on its own terms.  Four stars.

If you go by the HP manual it tells you how to uninstall the included memory, you don't do that but rather follow the instructions to install in the available slot in front of the installed memory. It took two tries to get the memory seated right, but when it was it booted up without problems to the \\"ready\\" state on the panel. I am running Windows 7 so followed the instructions to enable the memory in the user manual. This is where I hit a snag because there wasn't an automatic configuration prompt visible. There is a drop down menu where you can select total memory, but the highest option available is 256, and the total memory installed was 384. Ignoring this step I tried to troubleshoot by printing a configuration page, and the memory was listed as 384. I am telling this story for other non techies such as myself so if you run into the problems I did, print a configuration page to confirm your upgrade. HP tells you to do this in the manual, but only after you've enabled the memory. Only time will tell if the upgrade improves performance, but for $10 it's definitely worth it!

Generally, a reader in this genre is interested in understanding who SEALs are, what motivates them, how do they able to overcome extreme hardship and stress (physical and mental), how SEALs are trained, how are SEALs utilized in modern warfare, what first-hand insights can the author offer on the conflict, etc.  This book skips all over the place with no real organization.  There is also a lot of random information, seemingly irrelevant to the story, haphazardly scattered throughout the book.  There is very little detail provided on the training or missions, and the author seemingly takes a tone that the reader is somehow unworthy of such information.  I got the sense that the author was approached and influenced by some business-minded publishers/co-authors looking to cash in on the recent media interest surrounding the SEALs, and as a result, the book was hastily written and published.  The thing that was most troublesome was the author's view on his enemy, which has the effect of significantly undercutting the author's achievements.  The author makes broad generalizations about the Iraqi people, including by calling all insurgents \\"savages\\" and \\"terrorists.\\"  The author essentially likens his actions in Iraq to shooting fish in a barrel.  He tells a story about how his first kill was a woman who emerged from her home to lob a grenade at some Marines.  He dismisses her as a \\"savage,\\" but fails to consider her motivations and reactions to a foreign force invading her country and her town.  I understand that it was not the author's job to process those thoughts at the time, but I certainly would not go so far as to summarily dismiss the enemy and their tactics after the fact considering how far superior the coalition force was and the fact that it invaded those peoples' homes.  I would not think that most people would consider someone a terrorist just because they attempted to bring harm, by whatever means available, to a foreign invader.  It was also hard to overlook how egotistical and sociopathic the author comes across as.  It's obvious in the book, and unfortunate, that the author uses \\"patriotism\\" as a veil to disguise his true, seemingly insatiable, desire to play real life \\"cowboys and indians\\" out in the desert.  He repeatedly reminds the reader of how much of a \\"bad ass\\" he is by regaling the reader with several bar fight stories and hazing pranks that went too far.  He boasts how easy it was for him to kill, all the while touting that he is a good Christian.  The whole book just makes me feel ashamed as an American and seems to fly in the face of SEAL credo.

That's a resounding HELL YEAH!!  First, let me say it was nice to hear Klaus Meine sing the entirety of the songs. Usually in a LIVE show, he has the audience help quite a bit. This is okay, because that's what the fans love to do and what Klaus loves to hear. But, it was refreshing hearing his voice throughout the whole CD.  Second, let me say, the man with the bionic voice has never sounded better. He is in rare form and I love hearing these classics in his current voice, which barely sounds any different than his golden years. It's a superb feeling, hearing that incredible signature primal snarl once again on the rockers and then to turn around and hear that soft velvet voice, such as on Still Loving You, almost like he's whispering in your ear. It's like silk on skin. Hey, remember guys, I am a woman and call it like I hear it.  One thing I did ask myself at one point while listening to No One Like You was; is it LIVE or is it Memorex? For those too young to remember the commercial; it was an ad about a Memorex cassette tape being played up against the real singer and they asked in the ad, \\"is it LIVE or is it Memorex?\\" Okay, well, that is what I asked at first. Some of the guitar parts, especially the solos, were so exact, that I really wondered for a minute if the original guitar parts had literally been dubbed into the current song or did these guys actually channel their early years and remained so true to the originals that I really couldn't tell. However, I can assure you with certainty, that these songs have been redone, no dubbing included. Just listen and you can hear the difference between the new songs and the classic songs; there's a maturity, a more polished and refined sound, if you will. I also love the heavier sound they put to them; Rhythm of Love for instance. That really rocks.I am, to say the least, very impressed and am excited as hell over this CD. I still can't get over how Klaus sounds, and how Matthias attacks the Lead and James adding a fresh beat to the mix. I love the dual guitars, as usual the seamless mix of Schenker's and Jabs' guitar playing that is so cultured after all these years, gives it such a fine balance. I also want to say that I don't believe for a minute that Matthias or Rudolf would stand for dubbing the old guitars onto their new CD. They are proud of their abilities and so therefore, I am convinced that they are redoing these songs. Additionally, even though James and Pawel weren't members at the time of these classics, I have to say they hold their own. For the seasoned diehard Scorps fan, they can hear the difference in James' style compared to Herman's. And yet, James has added his own character, along with Pawel's bottom bass to make them new sounding. And they sound gooood! And I will always love Rudolf's rhythm guitar and his leads of course. He's so accomplished.  The other half of the CD is covers. Who ever thought they would do several covers on their album? Yeah sure, they have done covers before, but not to this extent.  I love Tainted Love. The way Klaus and the boys made it their own without losing the originality of the song, was most inspiring. Most of the time, when a band records covers, they make it so much their own, they screw up the song. Not so with Scorps. They probably realized how many times THEIR songs were covered and screwed up, so didn't want to make the same mistake. The lead solo by Matthias using that tremolo is a fantastic composition. And James' drums are hard driving fantastic!  Children of the Revolution is one heavy riffed song and it's one to really get into. It's heavier than what Scorpions have done in quite some time. Even though it was a cover, I was glad to hear Klaus' ability to still sing with that much strength. James is slamming out that beat that is so his form. And do we even have to mention the guitars? LOVE that heavy metal sound. But, guess what you hear at the end; that unmistakable signature sound of Matthias Jabs. If you were to listen to the original by T. Rex, you would find that there is no comparison. Scorpions killed this one and put the original to shame! I would love to see them add this to their final 2012-13 tour!  The choice of Across the Universe was a surprise to me as I have so many others in mind from The Beatles they could have done. But that's not my call. Listen, I'm not really complaining it's just not one of my favorite Beatle songs. Klaus, as usual, does it justice though, with that caressing smoothness of his voice, making the song so very much his own.  Tin Soldier starts out sort of poppy, but then suddenly it gets very \\"Scorpionized\\". It's almost like THEY wrote and did this song already. I liked it because of that familiar feeling of Golden Days Scorps. Actually, I'm really liking this song a lot.  All Day and All of the Night is fun for me because it's one I have loved since way back when. And for my favorite band to redo it is a double treat. What I find consistent in these covers is that they don't change it up so drastically that it loses the original sound. And yet Scorps still make it their own by adding to the songs, such things as, Matthias' distinguished lead guitar sound that put him in the annals of one of the best guitar players in the 80's, and what gave Scorpions their unique sound, and the inimitable, distinct raucous voice of the one and only Klaus Meine are still there. Of course there is James giving his own flavor along with Rudolf and Pawel. Other than that, the songs remain the same.  I like how they begin Ruby Tuesday. I couldn't quite put my finger on the guitar intro and why it sounded so familiar until I went and replayed both The Best Is Yet to Come and The Good Die Young intros. Yep, there it was that feeling of the two sort of mixed together for the Ruby Tuesday intro. I agree with a friend of mine who said the boys do this far better than the great Rolling Stones.  Scorpions do this song such justice, Stones should be proud. Matthias does quick little licks that are so characteristic; it's such a pleasure to hear this little bit of Scorps in an infamous cover song.  I highly recommend this excellently done CD. It's a wonderful trip into a classical rock tour; from their originals to covers that they excelled at in their execution. By the way, I put this on my MP3 player because I love listening to these splendid works in my ear while making people wonder why I have such a silly grin on my face. 0439023513|4.0|*No Spoilers*  Just finished Mockingjay. I am very impressed.  I read all three books with no interruption, feel the first of the trilogy is the strongest, but Mockingjay ends trilogy in a very powerful way.  Here are things I liked and didn't like in retrospect:  LIKED:  1) Katniss' choice between Gale and Peeta - The final choice in my opinion, was the best possible one.  2) Fate of Presidents of Coin and Snow - Considering what is revealed in this novel about each President's motives, I thought the outcome was very poetic and deserving on either side.  3) Graphic Content of novel - I generally don't feel gratuitous violence necessarily improves the telling of a story, but in this tale I felt the author was not afraid to let the senseless casualty of war become so overwhelmingly oppressive it forced me to stop trying to predict how her story was going to end, and just keep reading. Bold move.  4) It really wasn't a 'kid's' book (and certainly NOT a Twilight clone) - The harsh realities and ironies that occur throughout the story could bring out a reaction in anyone, young or old.  I think what I appreciate the most about finishing this trilogy is that discussions I might have afterwards would involve issues a lot deeper than romantic conclusions for Katniss, Gale, and Peeta. No one who reads these books can finish them without thinking about the actions that were taken on a deeper level. A very good thing.  DIDN'T LIKE:  1) Book three Katniss was almost as annoying as Bella Swan in Twilight - There were a lot of random closet hidings and impulsive decision making from Katniss in this part of the trilogy.  It was very irritating to read, and reminded me forcibly of Bella Swan in Twilight. The only saving grace is that in the end, it's hard for me to not to imagine Katniss devolving from a sure footed, smart, hunter type girl into a real emotional mess considering the horrific things she has had to deal with in so short a time. After going through not one but two hunger games in a row, only to see her most cherished relationships get even more hurt/damaged/put in jeopardy, perhaps the immaturity or selfish behavior is perfectly understandable. Still, it was very 'Bella Swan-ish' and I got very tired of Bella in Twilight. Thankfully (or sadly) things get so bad in this book, it replaces any irritation I had with Katniss for sympathy and concern, and sober reflection as to how I would have handled such horrors...  2) The sheer loss of life and characters - By the time the story was over, it was hard to imagine how the survivors of that human race could even continue to exist. Or maybe that was the point? I keep thinking perhaps the author wanted so many characters I grew to care about to die so her final thematic message would hit home all the more... no world that believes in sacrificing their own children to preserve some false sense of peace deserves any chance to exist in the first place...  Anyway, I liked the story overall and think it deserves to be read (and re-read.) The world is better for books like these that compel it's readers to think on a deeper level.  I was ready to dismiss this as just another fantasy outlet for Twilight or Harry Potter fans, but I am happy to say Hunger Games was much more substantial than that.

From hiding in Submarines, Ships, Mountains, Tunnels, the tactics used to survive are so real and believable. B003162R7E|4.0|Jimi Hendrix was by general agreement the most inventive, original and prolific musician/performer of his era, and carved out new territories for what could be done with an electric guitar. He composed and recorded more great material in five short years than do most musicians in a lifetime career ten times as long. He was imitated by thousands, but equalled by none.<br /><br />Following Jimi's untimely death in September 1970, a great volume of his hitherto unreleased material was put out first by Track Records, and later by Polydor. The first major posthumous release was `The Cry of Love' vinyl album (CD format didn't arrive on the market until the 1980s) released by Track Records in 1971 which contained, in running order:<br /><br />Side 1<br />1. Freedom<br />2. Drifting<br />3. Ezy Ryder<br />4. Night Bird Flying<br />5. My Friend<br /><br />Side 2<br />6. Straight Ahead<br />7. Astro man<br />8. Angel<br />9. In from the Storm<br />10. Belly Button Window<br /><br />`Cry of Love' can still be found on both the original vinyl and a later CD format, but good copies command high prices.<br /><br />`First Rays of the New Rising Sun', thought to be Hendrix's own chosen title for his planned 1971 album, contains all the above tracks, some digitally re-mastered (though it's hard to tell the difference) plus several bonus tracks originally released by Polydor in the mid-1970s variously on the albums `Rainbow Bridge,' `Experience' and `War Heroes':<br /><br />* Izabella<br />* Room Full of Mirrors<br />* Dolly Dagger<br />* Beginnings<br />* Stepping Stone<br />* Hey Baby (New Rising Sun)<br />* Earth Blues<br /><br />All these pieces with the exception of `My Friend' (a kind of late-night studio oddity from 1968 which didn't make the `Electric Ladyland' album) were composed and recorded in the spring and summer of 1970 and feature Brit-drummer Mitch Mitchell from the original `Experience' trio and Jimi's old army buddy Billy Cox on bass, who worked with Jimi in place of Noel Redding following the `Band of Gypsies' period and, it has to be said, was a more sensitive and just plain better musician than Redding. Overall it's pretty much the definitive collection of Hendrix's late-period material and a fine complement to his earlier finished products `Are You Experienced', `Axis Bold as Love' and the seminal 1968 epic `Electric Ladyland.'<br /><br />This late offering from the Sony `Legacy' collection claims to be \\"re-mastered\\" yet again, but it's hard to tell the difference and there is certainly no discernible improvement on the Polydor mix. As a \\"bonus\\" you get a 17-minute DVD, mostly of Eddie Kramer talking to camera between sound bites of Jimi playing. Basically, it's a waste of time and something of a gimmick, to make the Hendrix family \\"re-mastered re-release\\" of the re-mastered re-release of the re-release seem like something new and different. Please forgive the cynicism, but the music ain't better, it's the same. The music is 5-star and always will be, but somehow you just can't give full approval to a marketing gimmick like this which asks you to pay a lot extra for a short and  crummy DVD, so because of that, the rating drops a star.<br /><br />However, back to focus on the actual music: if you want to know why Jimi Hendrix is so highly revered by other rock musicians, listen to this collection and you will begin to understand. It's amazing to hear how fresh these recordings are: after 40 years they might have been recorded yesterday, they sound so contemporary; whilst so much music in the intervening period - especially from the 1980s - sounds old and dated. The musicianship here is exemplary, the compositions innovative, exciting and complex, the arrangements tight and disciplined, production values on the whole very high. What wonders Jimi might have produced as a more mature musician and composer we can only speculate: he was a true musician's musician, a one-off.<br /><br />BTW if you want to listen to `Cry of Love'as originally released in 1971, just mix the 10 tracks into the 1-10 order above and miss out the extra seven, crank up the volume and press `PLAY'. Isn't technology wonderful?gy wonderful?

B000002C2E|5.0|I'm someone who once considered this album overrated but who now considers it one of his best. I remember how I felt in 1997 when it came out. At that point I was enraptured by Dylan as a vocalist but felt he had pretty much lost it as a songwriter since his 1983 effort Infidels which I loved and pretty much everything before that - forgetting about the weaker tracks, as you do. In 1997 I just could not believe this won the Grammy ahead of OK Computer and thought there's is no way they could have actually properly listened to the Radiohead classic as it was so obviously so much better. I hadn't bought into the Dylan 'comeback' of Oh Mercy although I did like the track 'Series Of Dreams' a lot. i would say 'Oh Mercy was an improvement over the previous records that had been Dylan's nadir but still no way was he back to his best. Again, 'Under the Red sky' was ok but still not back to Infidels quality and before. The two covers albums both had tremendous songs on them such as Jim Jones - he still had the x factor, so to speak, albeit cover versions.  My early impression of 'Time Out of Mind' was that it had some tuneful tracks much like Oh Mercy and `Under the Red Sky' - those would be 'Love Sick', 'Cold Irons Bound' and 'Can't wait' and I ended up finding the follow up 'Love and Theft' to be better. I still think 'Mississippi' 'High Water', 'Honest With Me' and 'Po Boy' are genuine classics and the best of the late period song writing efforts. I found the production on Time Out Of Mind irritating and hardly ever listened to it, hitting bootlegs of live shows to hear new Dylan.  Something has happened though and I don't know what it is :) My appreciation of 'Time Out Of Mind' has soared. It is a mood piece. It is all about the blues. It has hit me very hard that this is consistently brilliant from beginning to end and is a totally unique album - an older man's record. It's not trying to rock out or find musical hooks. It's bluesy and chilled all the way with an incredible lyricist choosing a spoken word style of delivery, singing the blues from the heart. I now have no desire to hit the skip button - it doesn't even enter my head. Like 'Nashville Skyline' , 'Blood On the Tracks' and 'Infidels' we have a Dylan masterpiece where the album works as an album with the no skip thing going on. Comparing albums from different eras is perhaps pointless, but anyway, I don't think anything on here is as good as say 'Sad Eyed Lady Of The Lowlands', or whatever, but Blonde on Blonde had some lesser tracks and that is the thing about many a Dylan album. Unevenness caused by reaching high heights indeed. This ones is special when listened to as an album as a whole though and although 'Love and Theft' had some INCREDIBLE songs it is a touch uneven. TooM is a smooth, consistent listen.
